left;">\n<img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/40px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png" width="40" height="40" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/80px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="600" /> <a href="/wiki/2018_in_heavy_metal_music" title="2018 in heavy metal music">2018 in heavy metal music</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Heavy metal</b> (or simply <b>metal</b>) is a genre of <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> that developed in the late 1960s and early 1970s, largely in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> With roots in <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">blues rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-fast_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fast-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> the bands that created heavy metal developed a thick, massive sound, characterized by highly amplified <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">distortion</a>, extended <a href="/wiki/Guitar_solo" title="Guitar solo">guitar solos</a>, emphatic beats, and overall loudness. The genre\'s lyrics and performance styles are sometimes associated with <a href="/wiki/Aggression" title="Aggression">aggression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machismo" title="Machismo">machismo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fast_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fast-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1968, three of the genre\'s most famous pioneers, <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a> were founded.<sup id="cite_ref-Larson2004a_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2004a-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Though they came to attract wide audiences, they were often derided by critics. During the mid-1970s, <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a> helped spur the genre\'s evolution by discarding much of its <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> influence;<sup id="cite_ref-Walser_1993,_p._6_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walser_1993,_p._6-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Mot\xc3\xb6rhead">Mot\xc3\xb6rhead</a> introduced a <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> sensibility and an increasing emphasis on speed. Beginning in the late 1970s, bands in the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saxon_(band)" title="Saxon (band)">Saxon</a> followed in a similar vein. Before the end of the decade, heavy metal fans became known as "<a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_subculture" title="Heavy metal subculture">metalheads</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Headbanging" title="Headbanging">headbangers</a>".\n</p><p>During the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">glam metal</a> became popular with groups such as <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6tley_Cr%C3%BCe" title="M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce">M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poison_(American_band)" title="Poison (American band)">Poison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Def_Leppard" title="Def Leppard">Def Leppard</a>. <a href="/wiki/Underground_music" title="Underground music">Underground scenes</a> produced an array of more aggressive styles: <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">thrash metal</a> broke into the mainstream with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">Metallica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">Slayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">Megadeth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">Anthrax</a>, while other <a href="/wiki/Extreme_metal" title="Extreme metal">extreme subgenres of heavy metal</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">death metal</a> (with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">Death</a>, <a href="/wiki/Possessed_(band)" title="Possessed (band)">Possessed</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obituary_(band)" title="Obituary (band)">Obituary</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">black metal</a> (with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">Mayhem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bathory_(band)" title="Bathory (band)">Bathory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immortal_(band)" title="Immortal (band)">Immortal</a>) remain <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">subcultural</a> phenomena. Since the mid-1990s popular styles have further expanded the definition of the genre. These include <a href="/wiki/Groove_metal" title="Groove metal">groove metal</a> (with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Pantera" title="Pantera">Pantera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sepultura" title="Sepultura">Sepultura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God_(band)" title="Lamb of God (band)">Lamb of God</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">nu metal</a> (with bands such as <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">Korn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">Slipknot</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linkin_Park" title="Linkin Park">Linkin Park</a>), the latter of which often incorporates elements of <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">grunge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Characteristics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Musical_language"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Musical language</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-3"><a href="#Rhythm_and_tempo"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Rhythm and tempo</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-4"><a href="#Harmony"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Harmony</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a href="#Typical_harmonic_structures"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Typical harmonic structures</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-6"><a href="#Relationship_with_classical_music"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Relationship with classical music</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Lyrical_themes"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Lyrical themes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Image_and_fashion"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Image and fashion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Physical_gestures"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Physical gestures</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Fan_subculture"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Fan subculture</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Antecedents:_1950s_to_late_1960s"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Antecedents: 1950s to late 1960s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Origins:_late_1960s_and_early_1970s"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Origins: late 1960s and early 1970s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Mainstream:_late_1970s_and_1980s"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Mainstream: late 1970s and 1980s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Other_heavy_metal_genres:_1980s,_1990s,_and_2000s"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Other heavy metal genres: 1980s, 1990s, and 2000s</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#Thrash_metal"><span class="tocnumber">3.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Thrash metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Death_metal"><span class="tocnumber">3.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Death metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#Black_metal"><span class="tocnumber">3.4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Black metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-20"><a href="#Power_metal"><span class="tocnumber">3.4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Power metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#Doom_metal"><span class="tocnumber">3.4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Doom metal</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#1990s_and_early_2000s_subgenres_and_fusions"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">1990s and early 2000s subgenres and fusions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Recent_styles:_mid\xe2\x80\x93late_2000s_and_2010s"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Recent styles: mid\xe2\x80\x93late 2000s and 2010s</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Women_in_heavy_metal"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Women in heavy metal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>\n<p>Heavy metal is traditionally characterized by loud distorted guitars, emphatic rhythms, dense bass-and-drum sound, and vigorous vocals. Heavy metal subgenres variously emphasize, alter, or omit one or more of these attributes. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> critic <a href="/wiki/Jon_Pareles" title="Jon Pareles">Jon Pareles</a> writes, "In the taxonomy of popular music, heavy metal is a major subspecies of hard-rock\xe2\x80\x94the breed with less syncopation, less blues, more showmanship and more brute force."<sup id="cite_ref-JP_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JP-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The typical band lineup includes a <a href="/wiki/Drummer" title="Drummer">drummer</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_bass" title="Heavy metal bass">bassist</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_guitar" title="Rhythm guitar">rhythm guitarist</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">lead guitarist</a>, and a singer, who may or may not be an instrumentalist. <a href="/wiki/Keyboard_instruments" class="mw-redirect" title="Keyboard instruments">Keyboard instruments</a> are sometimes used to enhance the fullness of the sound.<sup id="cite_ref-W25_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W25-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Jon_Lord" title="Jon Lord">Jon Lord</a> played an overdriven <a href="/wiki/Hammond_organ" title="Hammond organ">Hammond organ</a>. In 1970, <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Jones_(musician)" title="John Paul Jones (musician)">John Paul Jones</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Moog_synthesizer" title="Moog synthesizer">Moog synthesizer</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_III" title="Led Zeppelin III">Led Zeppelin III</a></i>; by the 1990s, in "almost every subgenre of heavy metal" <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a> were used.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The band Judas Priest are onstage at a concert. From left to right are the singer, two electric guitarists, the bass player, and the drummer, who is seated behind a drumkit. The singer is wearing a black trenchcoat with metal studs." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/220px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/330px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/440px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a>, performing in 2005.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">electric guitar</a> and the sonic power that it projects through amplification has historically been the key element in heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-W23_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W23-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The heavy metal guitar sound comes from a combined use of high volumes and heavy <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Distortion (guitar)">distortion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> For classic heavy metal guitar tone, guitarists maintain moderate levels gain at moderate levels, without excessive preamp or pedal distortion, to retain open spaces and air in the music; the guitar amplifier is turned up loud to produce the characteristic "punch and grind".<sup id="cite_ref-Hodgson_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Thrash metal guitar tone has scooped mid-frequencies and <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_range_compression" title="Dynamic range compression">tightly compressed</a> sound with lots of bass frequencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodgson_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodgson-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guitar_solo" title="Guitar solo">Guitar solos</a> are "an essential element of the heavy metal code&#160;... that underscores the significance of the guitar" to the genre.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Most heavy metal songs "feature at least one guitar solo",<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> which is "a primary means through which the heavy metal performer expresses virtuosity".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Some exceptions are <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">nu metal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grindcore" title="Grindcore">grindcore</a> bands, which tend to omit guitar solos.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> With rhythm guitar parts, the "heavy crunch sound in heavy metal&#160;... [is created by] <a href="/wiki/Palm_muting" class="mw-redirect" title="Palm muting">palm muting</a>" the strings with the picking hand and using distortion.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Palm muting creates a tighter, more precise sound and it emphasizes the low end.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lead role of the guitar in heavy metal often collides with the traditional "frontman" or <a href="/wiki/Bandleader" title="Bandleader">bandleader</a> role of the vocalist, creating a musical tension as the two "contend for dominance" in a spirit of "affectionate rivalry".<sup id="cite_ref-W25_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W25-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Heavy metal "demands the subordination of the voice" to the overall sound of the band. Reflecting metal\'s roots in the 1960s counterculture, an "explicit display of emotion" is required from the vocals as a sign of authenticity.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Critic <a href="/wiki/Simon_Frith" title="Simon Frith">Simon Frith</a> claims that the metal singer\'s "tone of voice" is more important than the lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The prominent role of the bass is also key to the metal sound, and the interplay of bass and guitar is a central element. The bass guitar provides the low-end sound crucial to making the music "heavy".<sup id="cite_ref-W24_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W24-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The bass plays a "more important role in heavy metal than in any other genre of rock".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Metal basslines vary widely in complexity, from holding down a low <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">pedal point</a> as a foundation to doubling complex riffs and <a href="/wiki/Lick_(music)" title="Lick (music)">licks</a> along with the lead or rhythm guitars. Some bands feature the bass as a lead instrument, an approach popularized by Metallica\'s <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Burton" title="Cliff Burton">Cliff Burton</a> with his heavy emphasis on bass guitar solos and use of chords while playing bass in the early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lemmy" title="Lemmy">Lemmy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Mot\xc3\xb6rhead">Mot\xc3\xb6rhead</a> often played overdriven <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">power chords</a> in his bass lines.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The essence of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_drumming" title="Heavy metal drumming">heavy metal drumming</a> is creating a loud, constant beat for the band using the "trifecta of speed, power, and precision".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Heavy metal drumming "requires an exceptional amount of endurance", and drummers have to develop "considerable speed, coordination, and dexterity&#160;... to play the intricate patterns" used in  heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-Berry_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A characteristic metal drumming technique is the <a href="/wiki/Cymbal_choke" title="Cymbal choke">cymbal choke</a>, which consists of striking a cymbal and then immediately silencing it by grabbing it with the other hand (or, in some cases, the same striking hand), producing a burst of sound. The metal drum setup is generally much larger than those employed in other forms of rock music.<sup id="cite_ref-W24_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W24-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Black metal, death metal and some "mainstream metal" bands "all depend upon <a href="/wiki/Double_bass_drumming" class="mw-redirect" title="Double bass drumming">double-kicks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blast_beats" class="mw-redirect" title="Blast beats">blast beats</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Female musician Enid Williams from the band Girlschool and Lemmy Kilmeister from Mot\xc3\xb6rhead are shown onstage. Both are singing and playing bass guitar. A drumkit is seen behind them." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg/200px-Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg" width="200" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg/300px-Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg/400px-Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Girlschool_enid_and_lemmy-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Enid Williams from <a href="/wiki/Girlschool" title="Girlschool">Girlschool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lemmy" title="Lemmy">Lemmy</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Mot\xc3\xb6rhead">Mot\xc3\xb6rhead</a> singing "Please Don\'t Touch" live in 2009. The ties that bind the two bands started in the 1980s and were still strong in the 2010s.</div></div></div>\n<p>In live performance, <a href="/wiki/Loudness" title="Loudness">loudness</a>\xe2\x80\x94an "onslaught of sound", in sociologist <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">Deena Weinstein</a>\'s description\xe2\x80\x94is considered vital.<sup id="cite_ref-W23_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W23-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In his book <i>Metalheads</i>, psychologist Jeffrey Arnett refers to heavy metal concerts as "the sensory equivalent of war".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Following the lead set by <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>, early heavy metal acts such as <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">Blue Cheer</a> set new benchmarks for volume. As Blue Cheer\'s <a href="/wiki/Dickie_Peterson" title="Dickie Peterson">Dick Peterson</a> put it, "All we knew was we wanted more power."<sup id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> A 1977 review of a Mot\xc3\xb6rhead concert noted how "excessive volume in particular figured into the band\'s impact."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Weinstein makes the case that in the same way that <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melody</a> is the main element of <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop</a> and rhythm is the main focus of <a href="/wiki/House_music" title="House music">house music</a>, powerful sound, timbre, and volume are the key elements of metal. She argues that the loudness is designed to "sweep the listener into the sound" and to provide a "shot of youthful vitality".<sup id="cite_ref-W23_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W23-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Heavy metal performers tended to be almost exclusively male<sup id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87\xe2\x80\x9391_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87\xe2\x80\x9391-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> until at least the mid-1980s<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> apart from exceptions such as <a href="/wiki/Girlschool" title="Girlschool">Girlschool</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87\xe2\x80\x9391_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87\xe2\x80\x9391-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> However, by the 2010s women were making more of an impact,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and PopMatters\' Craig Hayes argues that metal "clearly empowers women".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In the sub-genres of symphonic and power metal, there has been a sizable number of bands that have had women as the lead singers; bands such as <a href="/wiki/Nightwish" title="Nightwish">Nightwish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delain" title="Delain">Delain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Within_Temptation" title="Within Temptation">Within Temptation</a> have featured women as lead singers with men playing instruments.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_language">Musical language</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhythm_and_tempo">Rhythm and tempo</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png/440px-Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png" width="440" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png 1.5x" data-file-width="613" data-file-height="184" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An example of a rhythmic pattern used in heavy metal. The upper stave is a <a href="/wiki/Palm_mute" title="Palm mute">palm-muted</a> <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_guitar" title="Rhythm guitar">rhythm guitar</a> part. The lower stave is the drum part.<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right"> <tbody><tr> <td style="width:1px"></td> <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio"> <div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" title="File:Heavy rythmic pattern audio.ogg">Rhythmic Pattern Audio</a></div> <div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="6.04589569161" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (136 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135599" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/ca/Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg/Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="216768" /></audio></div></div> <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div>\n<p>The rhythm in metal songs is emphatic, with deliberate stresses. Weinstein observes that the wide array of sonic effects available to metal drummers enables the "rhythmic pattern to take on a complexity within its elemental drive and insistency".<sup id="cite_ref-W24_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W24-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In many heavy metal songs, the main groove is characterized by short, two-note or three-note rhythmic figures\xe2\x80\x94generally made up of <a href="/wiki/Eighth_note" title="Eighth note">8th</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sixteenth_note" title="Sixteenth note">16th notes</a>. These rhythmic figures are usually performed with a <a href="/wiki/Staccato" title="Staccato">staccato</a> attack created by using a <a href="/wiki/Palm_mute" title="Palm mute">palm-muted</a> technique on the rhythm guitar.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Brief, abrupt, and detached <a href="/wiki/Rhythmic_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhythmic cell">rhythmic cells</a> are joined into rhythmic phrases with a distinctive, often jerky texture. These phrases are used to create rhythmic accompaniment and melodic figures called <a href="/wiki/Riff" class="mw-redirect" title="Riff">riffs</a>, which help to establish thematic <a href="/wiki/Hook_(music)" title="Hook (music)">hooks</a>. Heavy metal songs also use longer rhythmic figures such as <a href="/wiki/Whole_note" title="Whole note">whole note</a>- or dotted quarter note-length chords in slow-tempo <a href="/wiki/Power_ballad" class="mw-redirect" title="Power ballad">power ballads</a>. The tempos in early heavy metal music tended to be "slow, even ponderous".<sup id="cite_ref-W24_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W24-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> By the late 1970s, however, metal bands were employing a wide variety of tempos. In the 2000s decade, metal tempos range from slow ballad tempos (quarter note = 60 <a href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" class="mw-redirect" title="Beats per minute">beats per minute</a>) to extremely fast <a href="/wiki/Blast_beat" title="Blast beat">blast beat</a> tempos (quarter note = 350 beats per minute).<sup id="cite_ref-Berry_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berry-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony">Harmony</span></h4>\n<p>One of the signatures of the genre is the guitar <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">power chord</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In technical terms, the power chord is relatively simple: it involves just one main <a href="/wiki/Interval_(music)" title="Interval (music)">interval</a>, generally the <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifth</a>, though an <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> may be added as a doubling of the <a href="/wiki/Root_(chord)" title="Root (chord)">root</a>. When power chords are played on the lower strings at high volumes and with distortion, <a href="/wiki/Resultant_(organ)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resultant (organ)">additional low frequency sounds</a> are created, which add to the "weight of the sound" and create an effect of "overwhelming power".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Although the perfect fifth interval is the most common basis for the power chord,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> power chords are also based on different intervals such as the <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">minor third</a>, <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">major third</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">perfect fourth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diminished_fifth" class="mw-redirect" title="Diminished fifth">diminished fifth</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Minor_sixth" title="Minor sixth">minor sixth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Most power chords are also played with a consistent finger arrangement that can be slid easily up and down the <a href="/wiki/Fingerboard" title="Fingerboard">fretboard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Typical_harmonic_structures">Typical harmonic structures</span></h4>\n<p>Heavy metal is usually based on <a href="/wiki/Riff" class="mw-redirect" title="Riff">riffs</a> created with three main harmonic traits: modal scale progressions, tritone and chromatic progressions, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">pedal points</a>. Traditional heavy metal tends to employ modal scales, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_mode" title="Aeolian mode">Aeolian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_mode" title="Phrygian mode">Phrygian modes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Harmonically speaking, this means the genre typically incorporates modal chord progressions such as the Aeolian progressions I-\xe2\x99\xadVI-\xe2\x99\xadVII, I-\xe2\x99\xadVII-(\xe2\x99\xadVI), or I-\xe2\x99\xadVI-IV-\xe2\x99\xadVII and Phrygian progressions implying the relation between I and \xe2\x99\xadII (I-\xe2\x99\xadII-I, I-\xe2\x99\xadII-III, or I-\xe2\x99\xadII-VII for example). Tense-sounding <a href="/wiki/Chromatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromatic">chromatic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">tritone</a> relationships are used in a number of metal chord progressions.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MH_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MH-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In addition to using modal harmonic relationships, heavy metal also uses "pentatonic and blues-derived features".<sup id="cite_ref-Lilja_2009_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilja_2009-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The tritone, an interval spanning three whole tones\xe2\x80\x94such as C to F#\xe2\x80\x94was a forbidden dissonance in medieval ecclesiastical singing, which led monks to call it <i>diabolus in musica</i>\xe2\x80\x94"the devil in music".<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Heavy metal songs often make extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">pedal point</a> as a harmonic basis. A pedal point is a sustained tone, typically in the bass range, during which at least one foreign (i.e., dissonant) harmony is sounded in the other parts.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> According to Robert Walser, heavy metal harmonic relationships are "often quite complex" and the harmonic analysis done by metal players and teachers is "often very sophisticated".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In the study of heavy metal chord structures, it has been concluded that "heavy metal music has proved to be far more complicated" than other music researchers had realized.<sup id="cite_ref-Lilja_2009_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lilja_2009-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_classical_music">Relationship with classical music</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A guitarist, Ritchie Blackmore, is shown playing a Fender electric guitar onstage. He has long hair." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg/220px-Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="327" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">Ritchie Blackmore</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(rock_band)" title="Rainbow (rock band)">Rainbow</a>, known for the neoclassical approach in his guitar performances.</div></div></div>\n<p>Robert Walser stated that, alongside blues and R&amp;B, the "assemblage of disparate musical styles known&#160;... as \'<a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a><span style="padding-right:.2em;">\'</span>" has been a major influence on heavy metal since the genre\'s earliest days. Also that metal\'s "most influential musicians have been guitar players who have also studied classical music. Their appropriation and adaptation of classical models sparked the development of a new kind of guitar virtuosity [and] changes in the harmonic and melodic language of heavy metal."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an article written for <i><a href="/wiki/Grove_Music_Online" class="mw-redirect" title="Grove Music Online">Grove Music Online</a></i>, Walser stated that the "1980s brought on&#160;... the widespread adaptation of chord progressions and virtuosic practices from 18th-century European models, especially <a href="/wiki/J._S._Bach" class="mw-redirect" title="J. S. Bach">Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">Antonio Vivaldi</a>, by influential guitarists such as <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">Ritchie Blackmore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marty_Friedman" title="Marty Friedman">Marty Friedman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jason_Becker" title="Jason Becker">Jason Becker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uli_Jon_Roth" title="Uli Jon Roth">Uli Jon Roth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Van_Halen" title="Eddie Van Halen">Eddie Van Halen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Randy_Rhoads" title="Randy Rhoads">Randy Rhoads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yngwie_Malmsteen" title="Yngwie Malmsteen">Yngwie Malmsteen</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Kurt Bachmann of <a href="/wiki/Believer_(band)" title="Believer (band)">Believer</a> has stated that "If done correctly, metal and classical fit quite well together. Classical and metal are probably the two genres that have the most in common when it comes to feel, texture, creativity."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although a number of metal musicians cite classical composers as inspiration, classical and metal are rooted in different cultural traditions and practices\xe2\x80\x94classical in the <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">art music</a> tradition, metal in the <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> tradition. As <a href="/wiki/Musicologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Musicologists">musicologists</a> Nicolas Cook and Nicola Dibben note, "Analyses of popular music also sometimes reveal the influence of \'art traditions\'. An example is Walser\'s linkage of heavy metal music with the ideologies and even some of the performance practices of nineteenth-century <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>. However, it would be clearly wrong to claim that traditions such as blues, rock, heavy metal, rap or dance music derive primarily from "art music\'."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrical_themes">Lyrical themes</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_lyrics" title="Heavy metal lyrics">Heavy metal lyrics</a></div>\n<p>According to scholars David Hatch and Stephen Millward, Black Sabbath, and the numerous heavy metal bands that they inspired, have concentrated lyrically "on dark and depressing subject matter to an extent hitherto unprecedented in any form of pop music". They take as an example Sabbath\'s second album <i><a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(album)" title="Paranoid (album)">Paranoid</a></i> (1970), which "included songs dealing with personal trauma\xe2\x80\x94\'<a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Paranoid (Black Sabbath song)">Paranoid</a>\' and \'<a href="/wiki/Fairies_Wear_Boots" title="Fairies Wear Boots">Fairies Wear Boots</a>\' (which described the unsavoury side effects of drug-taking)\xe2\x80\x94as well as those confronting wider issues, such as the self-explanatory \'<a href="/wiki/War_Pigs" title="War Pigs">War Pigs</a>\' and \'<a href="/wiki/Hand_of_Doom_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Hand of Doom (Black Sabbath song)">Hand of Doom</a>\'."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Deriving from the genre\'s roots in blues music, sex is another important topic\xe2\x80\x94a thread running from Led Zeppelin\'s suggestive lyrics to the more explicit references of glam metal and nu metal bands.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two members from the band King Diamond are shown at a concert performance. From left to right are the singer and an electric guitarist. The singer has white and black face makeup and a top hat. Both are wearing black." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/220px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/330px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/440px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/King_Diamond_(band)" title="King Diamond (band)">King Diamond</a>, known for writing conceptual lyrics about horror stories.</div></div></div>\n<p>The thematic content of heavy metal has long been a target of criticism. According to <a href="/wiki/Jon_Pareles" title="Jon Pareles">Jon Pareles</a>, "Heavy metal\'s main subject matter is simple and virtually universal. With grunts, moans and subliterary lyrics, it celebrates&#160;... a party without limits&#160;... [T]he bulk of the music is stylized and formulaic."<sup id="cite_ref-JP_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JP-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Music critics have often deemed metal lyrics juvenile and banal, and others<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> have objected to what they see as advocacy of <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">misogyny</a> and the occult. During the 1980s, the <a href="/wiki/Parents_Music_Resource_Center" title="Parents Music Resource Center">Parents Music Resource Center</a> petitioned the U.S. Congress to regulate the popular music industry due to what the group asserted were objectionable lyrics, particularly those in heavy metal songs.<sup id="cite_ref-See,_e_2006_pp._104_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See,_e_2006_pp._104-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Andrew Cope states that claims that heavy metal lyrics are misogynistic are "clearly misguided" as these critics have "overlook[ed] the overwhelming evidence that suggests otherwise".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Music critic <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> called metal "an expressive mode [that] it sometimes seems will be with us for as long as ordinary white boys fear girls, pity themselves, and are permitted to rage against a world they\'ll never beat".<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Heavy metal artists have had to defend their lyrics in front of the U.S. Senate and in court. In 1985, <a href="/wiki/Twisted_Sister" title="Twisted Sister">Twisted Sister</a> frontman <a href="/wiki/Dee_Snider" title="Dee Snider">Dee Snider</a> was asked to defend his song "<a href="/wiki/Under_the_Blade_(song)" class="mw-redirect" title="Under the Blade (song)">Under the Blade</a>" at a U.S. Senate hearing. At the hearing, the <a href="/wiki/PMRC" class="mw-redirect" title="PMRC">PMRC</a> alleged that the song was about <a href="/wiki/Sadomasochism" title="Sadomasochism">sadomasochism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>; Snider stated that the song was about his bandmate\'s throat surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In 1986, <a href="/wiki/Ozzy_Osbourne" title="Ozzy Osbourne">Ozzy Osbourne</a> was sued over the lyrics of his song "<a href="/wiki/Suicide_Solution" title="Suicide Solution">Suicide Solution</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> A lawsuit against Osbourne was filed by the parents of John McCollum, a depressed teenager who committed suicide allegedly after listening to Osbourne\'s song. Osbourne was not found to be responsible for the teen\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 1990, Judas Priest was sued in American court by the parents of two young men who had shot themselves five years earlier, allegedly after hearing the subliminal statement "do it" in the song <a href="/wiki/Better_by_You,_Better_than_Me" title="Better by You, Better than Me">Better by You, Better than Me</a>, it was featured on the album <a href="/wiki/Stained_Class" title="Stained Class">Stained Class</a> (1978),<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> the song was also a <a href="/wiki/Spooky_Tooth" title="Spooky Tooth">Spooky Tooth</a> cover. While the case attracted a great deal of media attention, it was ultimately dismissed.<sup id="cite_ref-See,_e_2006_pp._104_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-See,_e_2006_pp._104-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1991, UK police seized death metal records from the British record label <a href="/wiki/Earache_Records" title="Earache Records">Earache Records</a>, in an "unsuccessful attempt to prosecute the label for obscenity".<sup id="cite_ref-Kahn-Harris_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kahn-Harris-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In some predominantly Muslim countries, heavy metal has been officially denounced as a threat to traditional values. In countries such as Morocco, Egypt, Lebanon, and Malaysia, there have been incidents of heavy metal musicians and fans being arrested and incarcerated.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In 1997, the Egyptian police jailed many young metal fans and they were accused of "devil worship" and blasphemy, after police found metal recordings during searches of their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-Kahn-Harris_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kahn-Harris-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> banned <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God_(band)" title="Lamb of God (band)">Lamb of God</a> from performing in their country, on the grounds that the "band\'s lyrics could be interpreted as being religiously insensitive" and blasphemous.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber,_Katherine_2013_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weber,_Katherine_2013-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Some people considered heavy metal music to being a leading factor for mental health disorders, and thought that heavy metal fans were more likely to suffer with a poor mental health, but study has proven that this is not true and the fans of this music have a lower or similar percentage of people suffering from poor mental health.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Image_and_fashion">Image and fashion</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_fashion" title="Heavy metal fashion">Heavy metal fashion</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The band Kiss is shown onstage at a concert. From left to right are the bassist Gene Simmons, two electric guitarists and the drummer, who is at the rear of the stage. Simmons is wearing spiked clothing and his tongue is extended. All members have white and black face makeup. Large guitar speaker stacks are shown behind the band." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/220px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/330px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/440px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> performing in 2004, wearing makeup.</div></div></div>\n<p>For many artists and bands, visual imagery plays a large role in heavy metal. In addition to its sound and lyrics, a heavy metal band\'s image is expressed in album cover art, logos, stage sets, clothing, design of instruments, and <a href="/wiki/Music_video" title="Music video">music videos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Down-the-back long hair is the "most crucial distinguishing feature of metal fashion".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Originally adopted from the hippie subculture, by the 1980s and 1990s heavy metal hair "symbolised the hate, angst and disenchantment of a generation that seemingly never felt at home", according to journalist Nader Rahman. Long hair gave members of the metal community "the power they needed to rebel against nothing in general".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The classic uniform of heavy metal fans consists of light colored, ripped frayed or torn blue jeans, black T-shirts, boots, and black leather or denim jackets. <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">Deena Weinstein</a> writes, "T-shirts are generally emblazoned with the logos or other visual representations of favorite metal bands."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In the 1980s, a range of sources, from punk and <a href="/wiki/Goth_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Goth music">goth music</a> to horror films, influenced metal fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-Umelec_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Umelec-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Many metal performers of the 1970s and 1980s used radically shaped and brightly colored instruments to enhance their stage appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_(2007),_p._135_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_(2007),_p._135-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blush_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blush-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fashion and personal style was especially important for glam metal bands of the era. Performers typically wore long, dyed, hairspray-teased hair (hence the nickname, "hair metal"); makeup such as lipstick and eyeliner; gaudy clothing, including leopard-skin-printed shirts or vests and tight denim, leather, or spandex pants; and accessories such as headbands and jewelry.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_(2007),_p._135_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_(2007),_p._135-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Pioneered by the heavy metal act <a href="/wiki/X_Japan" title="X Japan">X Japan</a> in the late 1980s, bands in the Japanese movement known as <a href="/wiki/Visual_kei" title="Visual kei">visual kei</a>\xe2\x80\x94which includes many nonmetal groups\xe2\x80\x94emphasize elaborate costumes, hair, and makeup.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_gestures">Physical gestures</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Image shows a band onstage with fans visible in the front of the picture. Some fans are raising their fists and others are raising their hands with the index finger and pinky extended." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/170px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg" width="170" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/255px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/340px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="907" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fans raise their fists and make the "devil horns" gesture at a <a href="/wiki/Metsat%C3%B6ll" title="Metsat\xc3\xb6ll">Metsat\xc3\xb6ll</a> concert.</div></div></div>\n<p>Many metal musicians when performing live engage in <a href="/wiki/Headbanging" title="Headbanging">headbanging</a>, which involves rhythmically beating time with the head, often emphasized by long hair. The <a href="/wiki/Corna" class="mw-redirect" title="Corna">il cornuto</a>, or devil horns, hand gesture was popularized by vocalist <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_James_Dio" title="Ronnie James Dio">Ronnie James Dio</a> while with Black Sabbath and <a href="/wiki/Dio_(band)" title="Dio (band)">Dio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MH_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MH-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Gene_Simmons" title="Gene Simmons">Gene Simmons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> claims to have been the first to make the gesture on the 1977 <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Gun" title="Love Gun">Love Gun</a></i> album cover, there is speculation as to who started the phenomenon.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Attendees of metal concerts do not dance in the usual sense. It has been argued that this is due to the music\'s largely male audience and "extreme heterosexualist ideology". Two primary body movements used are headbanging and an arm thrust that is both a sign of appreciation and a rhythmic gesture.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The performance of <a href="/wiki/Air_guitar" title="Air guitar">air guitar</a> is popular among metal fans both at concerts and listening to records at home.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">Deena Weinstein</a>, thrash metal concerts have two elements that are not part of the other metal genres: <a href="/wiki/Moshing" title="Moshing">moshing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stage_diving" title="Stage diving">stage diving</a>, which "were imported from the <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk/hardcore subculture</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_2009_228\xe2\x80\x93229_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein_2009_228\xe2\x80\x93229-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Weinstein states that moshing participants bump and jostle each other as they move in a circle in an area called the "pit" near the stage. Stage divers climb onto the stage with the band and then jump "back into the audience".<sup id="cite_ref-Weinstein_2009_228\xe2\x80\x93229_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstein_2009_228\xe2\x80\x93229-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fan_subculture">Fan subculture</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_subculture" title="Heavy metal subculture">Heavy metal subculture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HeavyMetalJckt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The back of a heavy metal fan wearing a denim jacket is shown. The jacket has patches and artwork for several heavy metal bands attached to the denim. The largest patch is for the band Metallica. It depicts a devil amidst flames." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/220px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/330px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/440px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="494" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HeavyMetalJckt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A heavy metal fan wearing a denim jacket with band patches and artwork of the heavy metal bands <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">Metallica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N&#39; Roses">Guns N\' Roses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">Slipknot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dio_(band)" title="Dio (band)">Dio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>It has been argued that heavy metal has outlasted many other rock genres largely due to the emergence of an intense, exclusionary, strongly masculine subculture.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> While the metal fan base is largely young, white, male, and blue-collar, the group is "tolerant of those outside its core demographic base who follow its codes of dress, appearance, and behavior".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Identification with the subculture is strengthened not only by the group experience of concert-going and shared elements of fashion, but also by contributing to metal magazines and, more recently, websites.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Attending live concerts in particular has been called the "holiest of heavy metal communions."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The metal scene has been characterized as a "subculture of alienation", with its own code of authenticity.<sup id="cite_ref-JQS_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JQS-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> This code puts several demands on performers: they must appear both completely devoted to their music and loyal to the subculture that supports it; they must appear uninterested in mainstream appeal and radio hits; and they must never "<a href="/wiki/Selling_out" title="Selling out">sell out</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">Deena Weinstein</a> states that for the fans themselves, the code promotes "opposition to established authority, and separateness from the rest of society".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Musician and filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Rob_Zombie" title="Rob Zombie">Rob Zombie</a> observes, "Most of the kids who come to my shows seem like really imaginative kids with a lot of creative energy they don\'t know what to do with" and that metal is "outsider music for outsiders. Nobody wants to be the weird kid; you just somehow end up being the weird kid. It\'s kind of like that, but with metal you have all the weird kids in one place".<sup id="cite_ref-Sam_Dunn_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sam_Dunn-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Scholars of metal have noted the tendency of fans to classify and reject some performers (and some other fans) as "<a href="/wiki/Poseur_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Poseur (music)">poseurs</a>" "who pretended to be part of the subculture, but who were deemed to lack authenticity and sincerity".<sup id="cite_ref-JQS_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JQS-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The origin of the term "heavy metal" in a musical context is uncertain. The phrase has been used for centuries in chemistry and metallurgy, where the periodic table organizes elements of both <a href="/wiki/Light_metal" title="Light metal">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metals</a> (e.g., uranium). An early use of the term in modern popular culture was by <a href="/wiki/Counterculture" title="Counterculture">countercultural</a> writer <a href="/wiki/William_S._Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs">William S. Burroughs</a>. His 1962 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Soft_Machine" title="The Soft Machine">The Soft Machine</a></i> includes a character known as "Uranian Willy, the Heavy Metal Kid". Burroughs\' next novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Nova_Express" title="Nova Express">Nova Express</a></i> (1964), develops the theme, using <i>heavy metal</i> as a metaphor for addictive drugs: "With their diseases and orgasm drugs and their sexless parasite life forms\xe2\x80\x94Heavy Metal People of Uranus wrapped in cool blue mist of vaporized bank notes\xe2\x80\x94And The Insect People of Minraud with metal music".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Inspired by Burroughs\' novels,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> the term was used in the title of the 1967 album <i>Featuring the Human Host and the Heavy Metal Kids</i> by <a href="/wiki/Hapshash_and_the_Coloured_Coat" title="Hapshash and the Coloured Coat">Hapshash and the Coloured Coat</a>, which has been claimed to be its first use in the context of music.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The phrase was later lifted by <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Pearlman" title="Sandy Pearlman">Sandy Pearlman</a>, who used the term to describe <a href="/wiki/The_Byrds" title="The Byrds">The Byrds</a> for their supposed "aluminium style of context and effect", particularly on their album <i><a href="/wiki/The_Notorious_Byrd_Brothers" title="The Notorious Byrd Brothers">The Notorious Byrd Brothers</a></i> (1968).<sup id="cite_ref-Dome_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dome-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Metal historian <a href="/wiki/Ian_Christe" title="Ian Christe">Ian Christe</a> describes what the components of the term mean in "<a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippiespeak</a>": "heavy" is roughly synonymous with "potent" or "profound," and "metal" designates a certain type of mood, grinding and weighted as with metal.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The word "heavy" in this sense was a basic element of <a href="/wiki/Beatnik" title="Beatnik">beatnik</a> and later <a href="/wiki/1960s_counterculture" class="mw-redirect" title="1960s counterculture">countercultural</a> <a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> <a href="/wiki/Slang" title="Slang">slang</a>, and references to "heavy music"\xe2\x80\x94typically slower, more amplified variations of standard pop fare\xe2\x80\x94were already common by the mid-1960s, such as in reference to <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge" title="Vanilla Fudge">Vanilla Fudge</a>. <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">Iron Butterfly</a>\'s debut album, released in early 1968, was titled <i><a href="/wiki/Heavy_(Iron_Butterfly_album)" title="Heavy (Iron Butterfly album)">Heavy</a></i>. The first use of "heavy metal" in a song lyric is in reference to a motorcycle in the <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">Steppenwolf</a> song "<a href="/wiki/Born_to_Be_Wild" title="Born to Be Wild">Born to Be Wild</a>", also released that year:<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> "I like smoke and lightning/Heavy metal thunder/Racin\' with the wind/And the feelin\' that I\'m under."\n</p><p>The first documented use of the phrase to describe a type of rock music identified to date appears in a review by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Gifford" title="Barry Gifford">Barry Gifford</a>. In the May 11, 1968, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, he wrote about the album <i><a href="/wiki/A_Long_Time_Comin%27" title="A Long Time Comin&#39;">A Long Time Comin\'</a></i> by U.S. band <a href="/wiki/Electric_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric Flag">Electric Flag</a>: "Nobody who\'s been listening to <a href="/wiki/Mike_Bloomfield" title="Mike Bloomfield">Mike Bloomfield</a>\xe2\x80\x94either talking or playing\xe2\x80\x94in the last few years could have expected this. This is the new soul music, the synthesis of white blues and heavy metal rock."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In January 1970 <a href="/wiki/Lucian_Truscott_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucian Truscott IV">Lucian K. Truscott IV</a> reviewing <i><a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_II" title="Led Zeppelin II">Led Zeppelin II</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/Village_Voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Village Voice">Village Voice</a> described the sound as "heavy" and made comparisons with <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">Blue Cheer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge" title="Vanilla Fudge">Vanilla Fudge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other early documented uses of the phrase are from reviews by critic <a href="/wiki/Mike_Saunders" title="Mike Saunders">Mike Saunders</a>. In the November 12, 1970 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, he commented on an album put out the previous year by the British band <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Humble Pie (band)">Humble Pie</a>: "<i><a href="/wiki/As_Safe_as_Yesterday_Is" title="As Safe as Yesterday Is">Safe as Yesterday Is</a>,</i> their first American release, proved that Humble Pie could be boring in lots of different ways. Here they were a noisy, unmelodic, heavy metal-leaden shit-rock band with the loud and noisy parts beyond doubt. There were a couple of nice songs&#160;... and one monumental pile of refuse". He described the band\'s latest, <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(album)" title="Humble Pie (album)">self-titled release</a> as "more of the same 27th-rate heavy metal crap".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a review of <a href="/wiki/Sir_Lord_Baltimore" title="Sir Lord Baltimore">Sir Lord Baltimore</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_Come_(Sir_Lord_Baltimore_album)" title="Kingdom Come (Sir Lord Baltimore album)">Kingdom Come</a></i> in the May 1971 <i><a href="/wiki/Creem" title="Creem">Creem</a></i>, Saunders wrote, "Sir Lord Baltimore seems to have down pat most all the best heavy metal tricks in the book".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Creem" title="Creem">Creem</a></i> critic <a href="/wiki/Lester_Bangs" title="Lester Bangs">Lester Bangs</a> is credited with popularizing the term via his early 1970s essays on bands such as Led Zeppelin and Black Sabbath.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Through the decade, <i>heavy metal</i> was used by certain critics as a virtually automatic putdown. In 1979, lead <i>New York Times</i> popular music critic <a href="/wiki/John_Rockwell" title="John Rockwell">John Rockwell</a> described what he called "heavy-metal rock" as "brutally aggressive music played mostly for minds clouded by drugs",<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and, in a different article, as "a crude exaggeration of rock basics that appeals to white teenagers".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Coined by <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a> drummer <a href="/wiki/Bill_Ward_(musician)" title="Bill Ward (musician)">Bill Ward</a>, "downer rock" was one of the earliest terms used to describe this style of music and was applied to acts such as Sabbath and <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock" title="Bloodrock">Bloodrock</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_Rock_(magazine)" title="Classic Rock (magazine)">Classic Rock</a></i> magazine described the downer rock culture revolving around the use of <a href="/wiki/Quaaludes" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaaludes">Quaaludes</a> and the drinking of wine.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Later the term would be replaced by "heavy metal".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Earlier on, as "heavy metal" emerged partially from the heavy psychedelic rock scene, also known as <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a>, "acid rock" was often used interchangeably with "heavy metal" and "hard rock". Musicologist Steve Waksman stated that "the distinction between acid rock, hard rock, and heavy metal can at some point never be more than tenuous",<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> while percussionist John Beck defined "acid rock" as synonymous with hard rock and heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Apart from \xe2\x80\x9cacid rock\xe2\x80\x9d, the terms "heavy metal" and "<a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">hard rock</a>" have often been used interchangeably, particularly in discussing bands of the 1970s, a period when the terms were largely synonymous.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> For example, the 1983 <i>Rolling Stone Encyclopedia of Rock &amp; Roll</i> includes this passage: "known for its aggressive blues-based hard-rock style, <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith" title="Aerosmith">Aerosmith</a> was the top American heavy-metal band of the mid-Seventies".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antecedents:_1950s_to_late_1960s">Antecedents: 1950s to late 1960s</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">Blues rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">garage rock</a></div>\n<p>Heavy metal\'s quintessential guitar style, built around distortion-heavy riffs and power chords, traces its roots to early 1950s <a href="/wiki/Memphis_blues" title="Memphis blues">Memphis blues</a> <a href="/wiki/Guitarist" title="Guitarist">guitarists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Hill_Louis" title="Joe Hill Louis">Joe Hill Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Johnson_(guitarist)" title="Willie Johnson (guitarist)">Willie Johnson</a>, and particularly <a href="/wiki/Pat_Hare" title="Pat Hare">Pat Hare</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-rolling_memphis_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rolling_memphis-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-palmer_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> who captured a "grittier, nastier, more ferocious electric guitar sound" on records such as <a href="/wiki/James_Cotton" title="James Cotton">James Cotton</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/File:James_Cotton_-_Cotton_Crop_Blues.ogg" title="File:James Cotton - Cotton Crop Blues.ogg">Cotton Crop Blues</a>" (1954);<sup id="cite_ref-palmer_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>  the late 1950s instrumentals of <a href="/wiki/Link_Wray" title="Link Wray">Link Wray</a>, particularly "<a href="/wiki/Rumble_(instrumental)" title="Rumble (instrumental)">Rumble</a>" (1958);<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> the early 1960s <a href="/wiki/Surf_music" title="Surf music">surf rock</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dick_Dale" title="Dick Dale">Dick Dale</a>, including "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go_Trippin%27" title="Let&#39;s Go Trippin&#39;">Let\'s Go Trippin\'</a>" (1961) and "<a href="/wiki/Misirlou" title="Misirlou">Misirlou</a>" (1962); and <a href="/wiki/The_Kingsmen" title="The Kingsmen">The Kingsmen</a>\'s version of "<a href="/wiki/Louie_Louie" title="Louie Louie">Louie Louie</a>" (1963) which made it a <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">garage rock</a> standard.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png" class="image"><img alt="The band Cream is shown playing on a TV show. From left to right are drummer Ginger Baker (sitting behind a drumkit with two bass drums) and two electric guitarists." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/220px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/330px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/440px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="498" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cream performing on the Dutch television program Fanclub in 1968.</div></div></div>\n<p>However, the genre\'s direct lineage begins in the mid-1960s. American blues music was a major influence on the early <a href="/wiki/British_rock" class="mw-redirect" title="British rock">British rockers</a> of the era. Bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Yardbirds" title="The Yardbirds">The Yardbirds</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">blues rock</a> by recording covers of classic blues songs, often speeding up the <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">tempos</a>. As they experimented with the music, the UK blues-based bands\xe2\x80\x94and the U.S. acts they influenced in turn\xe2\x80\x94developed what would become the hallmarks of heavy metal, in particular, the loud, distorted guitar sound.<sup id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">The Kinks</a> played a major role in popularising this sound with their 1964 hit "<a href="/wiki/You_Really_Got_Me" title="You Really Got Me">You Really Got Me</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to The Kinks\' <a href="/wiki/Dave_Davies" title="Dave Davies">Dave Davies</a>, other guitarists such as <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> and The Yardbirds\' <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck" title="Jeff Beck">Jeff Beck</a> were experimenting with feedback.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Where the blues rock drumming style started out largely as simple shuffle beats on small kits, drummers began using a more muscular, complex, and amplified approach to match and be heard against the increasingly loud guitar.<sup id="cite_ref-eyaqji_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Vocalists similarly modified their technique and increased their reliance on amplification, often becoming more stylized and dramatic. In terms of sheer volume, especially in live performance, The Who\'s "bigger-louder-wall-of-<a href="/wiki/Marshall_Amplification#Marshall_Stack" title="Marshall Amplification">Marshalls</a>" approach was seminal.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The combination of blues rock with <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a> formed much of the original basis for heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The variant or subgenre of psychedelic rock often known as "<a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a>" was particularly influential on heavy metal; acid rock is often defined as a heavier, louder, or harder variant of psychedelic rock,<sup id="cite_ref-AMgenre_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMgenre-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> or the more extreme side of the psychedelic rock genre, frequently containing a loud, improvised, and heavily distorted guitar-centered sound. Acid rock has been described as psychedelic rock at its "rawest and most intense," emphasizing the heavier qualities associated with both the positive and negative extremes of the <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_experience" title="Psychedelic experience">psychedelic experience</a> rather than only the idyllic side of psychedelia.<sup id="cite_ref-bisbort_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bisbort-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> American acid rock <a href="/wiki/Garage_bands" class="mw-redirect" title="Garage bands">garage bands</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/13th_Floor_Elevators" class="mw-redirect" title="13th Floor Elevators">13th Floor Elevators</a> epitomized the frenetic, heavier, darker and more psychotic sound of acid rock, a sound characterized by <a href="/wiki/Drone_(music)" title="Drone (music)">droning</a> guitar riffs, amplified feedback, and guitar distortion, while the 13th Floor Elevators\' sound in particular featured yelping vocals and "occasionally demented" lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-unterberger_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unterberger-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Frank Hoffman notes that: "Psychedelia was sometimes referred to as \'acid rock\'. The latter label was applied to a pounding, <a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">hard rock</a> variant that evolved out of the mid-1960s <a href="/wiki/Garage-punk" class="mw-redirect" title="Garage-punk">garage-punk</a> movement. ... When rock began turning back to softer, roots-oriented sounds in late 1968, acid-rock bands mutated into heavy metal acts."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the most influential bands in forging the merger of psychedelic rock and acid rock with the blues rock genre was the British power trio <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>, who derived a massive, heavy sound from <a href="/wiki/Unison" title="Unison">unison</a> riffing between guitarist <a
