href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> and bassist <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bruce" title="Jack Bruce">Jack Bruce</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Baker" title="Ginger Baker">Ginger Baker</a>\'s double bass drumming.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Their first two LPs, <i><a href="/wiki/Fresh_Cream" title="Fresh Cream">Fresh Cream</a></i> (1966) and <i><a href="/wiki/Disraeli_Gears" title="Disraeli Gears">Disraeli Gears</a></i> (1967), are regarded as essential prototypes for the future style of heavy metal. <a href="/wiki/The_Jimi_Hendrix_Experience" title="The Jimi Hendrix Experience">The Jimi Hendrix Experience</a>\'s debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Are_You_Experienced_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Are You Experienced (album)">Are You Experienced</a></i> (1967), was also highly influential. <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Hendrix</a>\'s virtuosic technique would be emulated by many metal guitarists and the album\'s most successful single, "<a href="/wiki/Purple_Haze" title="Purple Haze">Purple Haze</a>", is identified by some as the first heavy metal hit.<sup id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge" title="Vanilla Fudge">Vanilla Fudge</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge_(album)" title="Vanilla Fudge (album)">first album</a> also came out in 1967, has been called "one of the few American links between psychedelia and what soon became heavy metal",<sup id="cite_ref-vanillafudge_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanillafudge-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and the band has been cited as an early American heavy metal group.<sup id="cite_ref-browne_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-browne-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> On their self-titled debut album, Vanilla Fudge created "loud, heavy, slowed-down arrangements" of contemporary hit songs, blowing these songs up to "epic proportions" and "bathing them in a trippy, distorted haze."<sup id="cite_ref-vanillafudge_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vanillafudge-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the late 1960s, many psychedelic singers, such as <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Brown_(musician)" title="Arthur Brown (musician)">Arthur Brown</a>, began to create outlandish, theatrical and often <a href="/wiki/Macabre" title="Macabre">macabre</a> performances; which in itself became incredibly influential to many metal acts.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The American psychedelic rock band <a href="/wiki/Coven_(band)" title="Coven (band)">Coven</a>, who opened for early heavy metal influencers such as Vanilla Fudge and the Yardbirds, portrayed themselves as practitioners of <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">witchcraft</a> or <a href="/wiki/Black_magic" title="Black magic">black magic</a>, using dark\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a>\xe2\x80\x94imagery in their lyrics, album art, and live performances. Live shows consisted of elaborate, theatrical "<a href="/wiki/Black_Mass" title="Black Mass">Satanic rites</a>." Coven\'s 1969 debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Witchcraft_Destroys_Minds_%26_Reaps_Souls" title="Witchcraft Destroys Minds &amp; Reaps Souls">Witchcraft Destroys Minds &amp; Reaps Souls</a></i>, featured imagery of skulls, <a href="/wiki/Black_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Black mass">black masses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inverted_cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Inverted cross">inverted crosses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a> worship, and both the album artwork and the band\'s live performances marked the first appearances in rock music of the <a href="/wiki/Sign_of_the_horns" title="Sign of the horns">sign of the horns</a>, which would later become an important gesture in heavy metal culture.<sup id="cite_ref-people_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-people-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pattersonblackmetal_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pattersonblackmetal-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> At the same time in England, the band <a href="/wiki/Black_Widow_(band)" title="Black Widow (band)">Black Widow</a> were also among the first psychedelic rock bands to use occult and Satanic imagery and lyrics, though both Black Widow and Coven\'s lyrical and thematic influences on heavy metal were quickly overshadowed by the darker and heavier sounds of <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-people_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-people-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pattersonblackmetal_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pattersonblackmetal-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins:_late_1960s_and_early_1970s">Origins: late 1960s and early 1970s</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_(1970%27s).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two performers from Steppenwolf are shown in an onstage performance. From left to right are an electric guitarist (only the instrument is shown) and singer John Kay, who is swinging the microphone." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/170px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/255px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/340px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="660" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_(1970%27s).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John Kay of <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">Steppenwolf</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Critics disagree over who can be thought of as the first heavy metal band. Most credit either <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a>, with American commentators tending to favour Led Zeppelin and British commentators tending to favour Black Sabbath, though many give equal credit to both. A few commentators\xe2\x80\x94mainly American\xe2\x80\x94argue for other groups including <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">Iron Butterfly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">Steppenwolf</a> or <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">Blue Cheer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a>, the third band in what is sometimes considered the "unholy trinity" of heavy metal (Black Sabbath, Led Zeppelin, and Deep Purple), despite being slightly older than Black Sabbath and Led Zeppelin, fluctuated between many rock styles until late 1969 when they took a heavy metal direction.<sup id="cite_ref-Charlton_2003,_p._241_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charlton_2003,_p._241-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1968, the sound that would become known as heavy metal began to coalesce. That January, the San Francisco band <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">Blue Cheer</a> released a cover of <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cochran" title="Eddie Cochran">Eddie Cochran</a>\'s classic "<a href="/wiki/Summertime_Blues" title="Summertime Blues">Summertime Blues</a>", from their debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Vincebus_Eruptum" title="Vincebus Eruptum">Vincebus Eruptum</a></i>, that many consider the first true heavy metal recording.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The same month, <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">Steppenwolf</a> released its <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(Steppenwolf_album)" title="Steppenwolf (Steppenwolf album)">self-titled debut album</a>, including "<a href="/wiki/Born_to_Be_Wild" title="Born to Be Wild">Born to Be Wild</a>", which refers to "heavy metal thunder" in describing a motorcycle. In July, the <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeff Beck Group">Jeff Beck Group</a>, whose leader had preceded Page as The Yardbirds\' guitarist, released its debut record: <i><a href="/wiki/Truth_(Jeff_Beck_album)" title="Truth (Jeff Beck album)">Truth</a></i> featured some of the "most molten, barbed, downright funny noises of all time," breaking ground for generations of metal ax-slingers.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In September, Page\'s new band, <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, made its live debut in Denmark (billed as The New Yardbirds).<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>\' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_(album)" title="The Beatles (album)">White Album</a></i>, released the following month, included "<a href="/wiki/Helter_Skelter_(song)" title="Helter Skelter (song)">Helter Skelter</a>", then one of the heaviest-sounding songs ever released by a major band.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Pretty_Things" class="mw-redirect" title="The Pretty Things">The Pretty Things</a>\' <a href="/wiki/Rock_opera" title="Rock opera">rock opera</a> <i><a href="/wiki/S.F._Sorrow" title="S.F. Sorrow">S.F. Sorrow</a></i>, released in December, featured "proto heavy metal" songs such as "Old Man Going" and "I See You".<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">Iron Butterfly</a>\'s 1968 song  "<a href="/wiki/In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida" title="In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida">In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida</a>" is sometimes described as an example of the transition between <a href="/wiki/Acid_rock" title="Acid rock">acid rock</a> and heavy metal<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERood19946_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERood19946-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> or the turning point in which acid rock became "heavy metal",<sup id="cite_ref-houstonpress_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houstonpress-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and both Iron Butterfly\'s 1968 album <a href="/wiki/In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida_(album)" title="In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida (album)"><i>In-A-Gadda-Da-Vida</i></a> and Blue Cheer\'s 1968 album <i>Vincebus Eruptum</i> have been described as laying the foundation of heavy metal and greatly influential in the transformation of acid rock into heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture</a> period <a href="/wiki/MC5" title="MC5">MC5</a>, who began as part of the Detroit garage rock scene, developed a raw distorted style that has been seen as a major influence on the future sound of both heavy metal and later punk music.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">The Stooges</a> also began to establish and influence a heavy metal and later punk sound, with songs such as "<a href="/wiki/I_Wanna_Be_Your_Dog" title="I Wanna Be Your Dog">I Wanna Be Your Dog</a>", featuring pounding and distorted heavy guitar power chord riffs.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a> released two of their heaviest and loudest songs to date; "<a href="/wiki/Ibiza_Bar" title="Ibiza Bar">Ibiza Bar</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Nile_Song" title="The Nile Song">The Nile Song</a>", which was regarded as "one of the heaviest songs the band recorded".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/King_Crimson" title="King Crimson">King Crimson</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/In_the_Court_of_the_Crimson_King" title="In the Court of the Crimson King">debut album</a> started with "<a href="/wiki/21st_Century_Schizoid_Man" title="21st Century Schizoid Man">21st Century Schizoid Man</a>," which was considered heavy metal by several critics.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A colour photograph of the four members of Led Zeppelin performing onstage, with some other figures visible in the background. The band members shown are, from left to right, the bassist, drummer, guitarist, and lead singer. Large guitar speaker stacks are behind the band members." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/220px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/330px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/440px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From left to <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a> performing at <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Stadium" title="Chicago Stadium">Chicago Stadium</a> in January 1975</div></div></div>\n<p>In January 1969, Led Zeppelin\'s <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_(album)" title="Led Zeppelin (album)">self-titled debut album</a> was released and reached number 10 on the <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> album chart. In July, Zeppelin and a power trio with a Cream-inspired, but cruder sound, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Funk_Railroad" title="Grand Funk Railroad">Grand Funk Railroad</a>, played the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_International_Pop_Festival_(1969)" title="Atlanta International Pop Festival (1969)">Atlanta Pop Festival</a>. That same month, another Cream-rooted trio led by <a href="/wiki/Leslie_West" title="Leslie West">Leslie West</a> released <i><a href="/wiki/Mountain_(Leslie_West_album)" title="Mountain (Leslie West album)">Mountain</a></i>, an album filled with heavy blues rock guitar and roaring vocals. In August, the group\xe2\x80\x94now itself dubbed <a href="/wiki/Mountain_(band)" title="Mountain (band)">Mountain</a>\xe2\x80\x94played an hour-long set at the <a href="/wiki/Woodstock_Festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Woodstock Festival">Woodstock Festival</a>, exposing the crowd of 300,000 people to the emerging sound of heavy metal.<sup id="cite_ref-prown_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prown-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Mountain\'s proto-metal or early heavy metal hit song "<a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Queen" title="Mississippi Queen">Mississippi Queen</a>" from the album <i><a href="/wiki/Climbing!" title="Climbing!">Climbing!</a></i> is especially credited with paving the way for heavy metal and was one of the first heavy guitar songs to receive regular play on radio.<sup id="cite_ref-prown_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prown-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hoffmann_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoffmann-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-axs_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axs-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In September 1969, the Beatles released the album <i><a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road" title="Abbey Road">Abbey Road</a></i> containing the track "<a href="/wiki/I_Want_You_(She%27s_So_Heavy)" title="I Want You (She&#39;s So Heavy)">I Want You (She\'s So Heavy)</a>" which has been credited as an early example of or influence on heavy metal or <a href="/wiki/Doom_metal" title="Doom metal">doom metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In October 1969, British band <a href="/wiki/High_Tide_(band)" title="High Tide (band)">High Tide</a> debuted with the heavy, proto-metal album <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_Shanties_(High_Tide_album)" title="Sea Shanties (High Tide album)">Sea Shanties</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Allmusic_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allmusic-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-houstonpress_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houstonpress-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Led Zeppelin defined central aspects of the emerging genre, with Page\'s highly distorted guitar style and singer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Plant" title="Robert Plant">Robert Plant</a>\'s dramatic, wailing vocals.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Other bands, with a more consistently heavy, "purely" metal sound, would prove equally important in codifying the genre. The 1970 releases by <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">Black Sabbath</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath_(album)" title="Black Sabbath (album)">Black Sabbath</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(album)" title="Paranoid (album)">Paranoid</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">Deep Purple</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/In_Rock_(Deep_Purple_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="In Rock (Deep Purple album)">In Rock</a></i>) were crucial in this regard.<sup id="cite_ref-eyaqji_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg" title="File:Led Zeppelin Whole Lotta Love.ogg">"Whole Lotta Love"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="30.000272108844" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f2/Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (67 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="67022" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/f/f2/Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg/Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="200888" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/Whole_Lotta_Love" title="Whole Lotta Love">Whole Lotta Love</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, from <i><a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_II" title="Led Zeppelin II">Led Zeppelin II</a></i> (1969). The heavy riff-based song, using lyrics culled from the blues.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup></div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>\'s Black Sabbath had developed a particularly heavy sound in part due to an <a href="/wiki/Industrial_accident" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial accident">industrial accident</a> guitarist <a href="/wiki/Tony_Iommi" title="Tony Iommi">Tony Iommi</a> suffered before cofounding the band. Unable to play normally, Iommi had to tune his guitar down for easier fretting and rely on power chords with their relatively simple fingering.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> The bleak, industrial, <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> environment of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> city full of noisy <a href="/wiki/Factories" class="mw-redirect" title="Factories">factories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a>, has itself been credited with influencing Black Sabbath\'s heavy, chugging, metallic sound and the sound of heavy metal in general.<sup id="cite_ref-allsop_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allsop-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wood_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newyorker_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birmingham_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birmingham-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Deep Purple had fluctuated between styles in its early years, but by 1969 vocalist <a href="/wiki/Ian_Gillan" title="Ian Gillan">Ian Gillan</a> and guitarist <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">Ritchie Blackmore</a> had led the band toward the developing heavy metal style.<sup id="cite_ref-Charlton_2003,_p._241_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charlton_2003,_p._241-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In 1970, Black Sabbath and Deep Purple scored major UK chart hits with "<a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Paranoid (Black Sabbath song)">Paranoid</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Black_Night" title="Black Night">Black Night</a>", respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-RnR_Hall_of_Fame_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RnR_Hall_of_Fame-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> That same year, two other British bands released debut albums in a heavy metal mode: <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Heep_(band)" title="Uriah Heep (band)">Uriah Heep</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Very_%27Eavy..._Very_%27Umble" class="mw-redirect" title="Very &#39;Eavy... Very &#39;Umble">Very \'Eavy... Very \'Umble</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/UFO_(band)" title="UFO (band)">UFO</a> with <i><a href="/wiki/Unidentified_Flying_Object_(album)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unidentified Flying Object (album)">UFO 1</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock" title="Bloodrock">Bloodrock</a> released their <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock_(album)" title="Bloodrock (album)">self-titled debut album</a>, containing a collection of heavy guitar riffs, gruff style vocals and sadistic and macabre lyrics.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The influential <a href="/wiki/Budgie_(band)" title="Budgie (band)">Budgie</a> brought the new metal sound into a power trio context, creating some of the heaviest music of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The occult lyrics and imagery employed by Black Sabbath and Uriah Heep would prove particularly influential; Led Zeppelin also began foregrounding such elements with its <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_IV" title="Led Zeppelin IV">fourth album</a>, released in 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> In 1973, Deep Purple released the song "<a href="/wiki/Smoke_on_the_Water" title="Smoke on the Water">Smoke on the Water</a>", with the iconic riff that\'s usually considered as the most recognizable one in "heavy rock" history, as a single of the classic live album <a href="/wiki/Made_in_Japan_(Deep_Purple_album)" title="Made in Japan (Deep Purple album)">Made in Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three members of the band Thin Lizzy are shown onstage. From left to right are a guitarist, bass player, and another electric guitarist. Both electric guitarists have long hair." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/170px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/255px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/340px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brian_Robertson_(guitarist)" title="Brian Robertson (guitarist)">Brian Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott" title="Phil Lynott">Phil Lynott</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Gorham" title="Scott Gorham">Scott Gorham</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy" title="Thin Lizzy">Thin Lizzy</a> performing during the Bad Reputation Tour, November 24, 1977.</div></div></div>\n<p>On the other side of the Atlantic, the trend-setting group was <a href="/wiki/Grand_Funk_Railroad" title="Grand Funk Railroad">Grand Funk Railroad</a>, described as "the most commercially successful American heavy-metal band from 1970 until they disbanded in 1976, [they] established the Seventies success formula: continuous touring".<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Other influential bands identified with metal emerged in the U.S., such as <a href="/wiki/Sir_Lord_Baltimore" title="Sir Lord Baltimore">Sir Lord Baltimore</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_Come_(Sir_Lord_Baltimore_album)" title="Kingdom Come (Sir Lord Baltimore album)">Kingdom Come</a>,</i> 1970), <a href="/wiki/Blue_%C3%96yster_Cult" title="Blue \xc3\x96yster Cult">Blue \xc3\x96yster Cult</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Blue_%C3%96yster_Cult_(album)" title="Blue \xc3\x96yster Cult (album)">Blue \xc3\x96yster Cult</a></i>, 1972), <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith" title="Aerosmith">Aerosmith</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Aerosmith_(album)" title="Aerosmith (album)">Aerosmith</a></i>, 1973) and <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">Kiss</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Kiss_(Kiss_album)" title="Kiss (Kiss album)">Kiss</a></i>, 1974). Sir Lord Baltimore\'s 1970 debut album and both <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Humble Pie (band)">Humble Pie</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/As_Safe_As_Yesterday_Is" class="mw-redirect" title="As Safe As Yesterday Is">debut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(album)" title="Humble Pie (album)">self-titled third album</a> were all among the first albums to be described in print as "heavy metal", with <i><a href="/wiki/As_Safe_As_Yesterday_Is" class="mw-redirect" title="As Safe As Yesterday Is">As Safe As Yesterday Is</a></i> being referred to by the term "heavy metal" in a 1970 review in <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Various smaller bands from the U.S., U.K, and Continental Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Bang_(American_band)" title="Bang (American band)">Bang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josefus" title="Josefus">Josefus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leaf_Hound" title="Leaf Hound">Leaf Hound</a>, <a href="/wiki/Primevil_(band)" title="Primevil (band)">Primeval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hard_Stuff" title="Hard Stuff">Hard Stuff</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Janey" title="Truth and Janey">Truth and Janey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dust_(band)" title="Dust (band)">Dust</a>, <a href="/wiki/JPT_Scare_Band" title="JPT Scare Band">JPT Scare Band</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frijid_Pink" title="Frijid Pink">Frijid Pink</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cactus_(American_band)" title="Cactus (American band)">Cactus</a>, <a href="/wiki/May_Blitz" title="May Blitz">May Blitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Captain_Beyond" title="Captain Beyond">Captain Beyond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toad_(band)" title="Toad (band)">Toad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Granicus_(band)" title="Granicus (band)">Granicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Claw_(band)" title="Iron Claw (band)">Iron Claw</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yesterday%27s_Children" title="Yesterday&#39;s Children">Yesterday\'s Children</a>, though lesser known outside of their respective scenes, proved to be greatly influential on the emerging metal movement. In Germany, <a href="/wiki/Scorpions_(band)" title="Scorpions (band)">Scorpions</a> debuted with <i><a href="/wiki/Lonesome_Crow" title="Lonesome Crow">Lonesome Crow</a></i> in 1972. Blackmore, who had emerged as a virtuoso soloist with Deep Purple\'s highly influential album <i><a href="/wiki/Machine_Head_(album)" title="Machine Head (album)">Machine Head</a></i> (1972), left the band in 1975 to form <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(rock_band)" title="Rainbow (rock band)">Rainbow</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_James_Dio" title="Ronnie James Dio">Ronnie James Dio</a>, singer and bassist for blues rock band <a href="/wiki/Elf_(band)" title="Elf (band)">Elf</a> and future vocalist for Black Sabbath and heavy metal band <a href="/wiki/Dio_(band)" title="Dio (band)">Dio</a>. Rainbow with Ronnie James Dio would expand on the mystical and <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a>-based lyrics and themes sometimes found in heavy metal, pioneering both <a href="/wiki/Power_metal" title="Power metal">power metal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_metal" title="Neoclassical metal">neoclassical metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eduardo_Rivadavia_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eduardo_Rivadavia-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> These bands also built audiences via constant touring and increasingly elaborate stage shows.<sup id="cite_ref-eyaqji_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As described above, there are arguments about whether these and other early bands truly qualify as "heavy metal" or simply as "hard rock". Those closer to the music\'s blues roots or placing greater emphasis on melody are now commonly ascribed the latter label. <a href="/wiki/AC/DC" title="AC/DC">AC/DC</a>, which debuted with <i><a href="/wiki/High_Voltage_(Australian_album)" class="mw-redirect" title="High Voltage (Australian album)">High Voltage</a></i> in 1975, is a prime example. The 1983 <i>Rolling Stone</i> encyclopedia entry begins, "Australian heavy-metal band AC/DC".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Rock historian Clinton Walker writes, "Calling AC/DC a heavy metal band in the seventies was as inaccurate as it is today.... [They] were a rock \'n\' roll band that just happened to be heavy enough for metal".<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The issue is not only one of shifting definitions, but also a persistent distinction between musical style and audience identification: Ian Christe describes how the band "became the stepping-stone that led huge numbers of hard rock fans into heavy metal perdition".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In certain cases, there is little debate. After Black Sabbath, the next major example is Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">Judas Priest</a>, which debuted with <i><a href="/wiki/Rocka_Rolla" title="Rocka Rolla">Rocka Rolla</a></i> in 1974. In Christe\'s description,\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>"Black Sabbath\'s audience was...left to scavenge for sounds with similar impact. By the mid-1970s, heavy metal aesthetic could be spotted, like a mythical beast, in the moody bass and complex dual guitars of <a href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy" title="Thin Lizzy">Thin Lizzy</a>, in the stagecraft of <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">Alice Cooper</a>, in the sizzling guitar and showy vocals of <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a>, and in the thundering medieval questions of Rainbow.... Judas Priest arrived to unify and amplify these diverse highlights from hard rock\'s sonic palette. For the first time, heavy metal became a true genre unto itself."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Though Judas Priest did not have a top 40 album in the United States until 1980, for many it was the definitive post-Sabbath heavy metal band; its twin-guitar attack, featuring rapid tempos and a non-<a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">bluesy</a>, more cleanly metallic sound, was a major influence on later acts.<sup id="cite_ref-Walser_1993,_p._6_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walser_1993,_p._6-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> While heavy metal was growing in popularity, most critics were not enamored of the music. Objections were raised to metal\'s adoption of visual spectacle and other trappings of commercial artifice,<sup id="cite_ref-gnwtey_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gnwtey-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> but the main offense was its perceived musical and lyrical vacuity: reviewing a Black Sabbath album in the early 1970s, leading critic <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> described it as "dull and decadent...dim-witted, amoral exploitation."<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream:_late_1970s_and_1980s">Mainstream: late 1970s and 1980s</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Four members of Iron Maiden are shown in concert. From left to right are a bass guitarist and then three electric guitarists. All members shown have long hair." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/220px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/330px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/440px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1437" data-file-height="1157" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, one of the central bands in the <a href="/wiki/New_wave_of_British_heavy_metal" title="New wave of British heavy metal">new wave of British heavy metal</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">Punk rock</a> emerged in the mid-1970s as a reaction against contemporary social conditions as well as what was perceived as the overindulgent, overproduced rock music of the time, including heavy metal. Sales of heavy metal records declined sharply in the late 1970s in the face of punk, <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a>, and more mainstream rock.<sup id="cite_ref-gnwtey_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gnwtey-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> With the major labels fixated on punk, many newer British heavy metal bands were inspired by the movement\'s aggressive, high-energy sound and "<a href="/wiki/Low_fidelity" class="mw-redirect" title="Low fidelity">lo-fi</a>", <a href="/wiki/Do_it_yourself" title="Do it yourself">do it yourself</a> ethos. Underground metal bands began putting out cheaply recorded releases independently to small, devoted audiences.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Mot\xc3\xb6rhead">Mot\xc3\xb6rhead</a>, founded in 1975, was the first important band to straddle the punk/metal divide. With the explosion of punk in 1977, others followed. British music papers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sounds_(magazine)" title="Sounds (magazine)">Sounds</a></i> took notice, with <i>Sounds</i> writer Geoff Barton christening the movement the "New Wave of British Heavy Metal".<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> NWOBHM bands including <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxon_(band)" title="Saxon (band)">Saxon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Def_Leppard" title="Def Leppard">Def Leppard</a> re-energized the heavy metal genre. Following the lead set by Judas Priest and Mot\xc3\xb6rhead, they toughened up the sound, reduced its blues elements, and emphasized increasingly fast tempos.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1980, the NWOBHM had broken into the mainstream, as albums by Iron Maiden and Saxon, as well as Mot\xc3\xb6rhead, reached the British top 10. Though less commercially successful, other NWOBHM bands such as <a href="/wiki/Venom_(band)" title="Venom (band)">Venom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Head_(band)" title="Diamond Head (band)">Diamond Head</a> would have a significant influence on metal\'s development.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In 1981, Mot\xc3\xb6rhead became the first of this new breed of metal bands to top the UK charts with the live album <i><a href="/wiki/No_Sleep_%27til_Hammersmith" title="No Sleep &#39;til Hammersmith">No Sleep \'til Hammersmith</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-RC1_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RC1-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first generation of metal bands was ceding the limelight. Deep Purple had broken up soon after Blackmore\'s departure in 1975, and Led Zeppelin broke up following drummer <a href="/wiki/John_Bonham" title="John Bonham">John Bonham</a>\'s death in 1980. Black Sabbath plagued with infighting and substance abuse, while facing fierce competition with their opening band, the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> band <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen" title="Van Halen">Van Halen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Van_Halen" title="Eddie Van Halen">Eddie Van Halen</a> established himself as one of the leading metal guitarists of the era. His solo on "<a href="/wiki/Eruption_(instrumental)" title="Eruption (instrumental)">Eruption</a>", from the band\'s <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen_(album)" title="Van Halen (album)">self-titled 1978 album</a>, is considered a milestone.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>  Eddie Van Halen\'s sound even crossed over into pop music when his guitar solo was featured on the track "<a href="/wiki/Beat_It" title="Beat It">Beat It</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a> (a U.S. number 1 in February 1983).<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Inspired by Van Halen\'s success, a metal scene began to develop in Southern California during the late 1970s. Based on the clubs of L.A.\'s <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Strip" title="Sunset Strip">Sunset Strip</a>, bands such as <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Riot" title="Quiet Riot">Quiet Riot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ratt" title="Ratt">Ratt</a>, <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6tley_Cr%C3%BCe" title="M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce">M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/W.A.S.P._(band)" title="W.A.S.P. (band)">W.A.S.P.</a> were influenced by traditional heavy metal of the earlier 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> These acts incorporated the theatrics (and sometimes makeup) of <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">glam metal</a> or "hair metal" such as <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">Alice Cooper</a> and Kiss.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Glam metal bands were often visually distinguished by long, overworked hair styles accompanied by wardrobes which were sometimes considered cross-gender. The lyrics of these <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">glam metal</a> bands characteristically emphasized <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">hedonism</a> and wild behavior, including lyrics which involved sexual expletives and the use of narcotics.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_Purgatory_29_second_file.ogg" title="File:Iron Maiden Purgatory 29 second file.ogg">"Purgatory"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.320408163265" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Iron_Maiden_Purgatory_29_second_file.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/92/Iron_Maiden_Purgatory_29_second_file.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (99 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="99411" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/9/92/Iron_Maiden_Purgatory_29_second_file.ogg/Iron_Maiden_Purgatory_29_second_file.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="209592" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/Purgatory_(song)" title="Purgatory (song)">Purgatory</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a>, from the album <i><a href="/wiki/Killers_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Killers (Iron Maiden album)">Killers</a></i> (1981). The early Iron Maiden sound was a mix of punk rock speed and heavy metal guitar work typical of the new wave of British heavy metal.</div></div><hr /><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Van_Halen_Hot_for_Teacher.ogg" title="File:Van Halen Hot for Teacher.ogg">"Hot for Teacher"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_3" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="27.776598639456" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Van_Halen_Hot_for_Teacher.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/0e/Van_Halen_Hot_for_Teacher.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (64 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="64368" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/0/0e/Van_Halen_Hot_for_Teacher.ogg/Van_Halen_Hot_for_Teacher.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="189056" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Sample of "<a href="/wiki/Hot_for_Teacher" title="Hot for Teacher">Hot for Teacher</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen" title="Van Halen">Van Halen</a>, from the album <i><a href="/wiki/1984_(Van_Halen_album)" title="1984 (Van Halen album)">1984</a></i> (1984). This sample demonstrates their sound\'s similarity to the glam metal style.</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In the wake of the new wave of British heavy metal and Judas Priest\'s breakthrough <i><a href="/wiki/British_Steel_(album)" title="British Steel (album)">British Steel</a></i> (1980), heavy metal became increasingly popular in the early 1980s. Many metal artists benefited from the exposure they received on <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">MTV</a>, which began airing in 1981\xe2\x80\x94sales often soared if a band\'s videos screened on the channel.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Def Leppard\'s videos for <i><a href="/wiki/Pyromania_(album)" title="Pyromania (album)">Pyromania</a></i> (1983) made them superstars in America and Quiet Riot became the first domestic heavy metal band to top the <i>Billboard</i> chart with <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_Health" title="Metal Health">Metal Health</a></i> (1983). One of the seminal events in metal\'s growing popularity was the 1983 <a href="/wiki/US_Festival" title="US Festival">US Festival</a> in California, where the "heavy metal day" featuring Ozzy Osbourne, Van Halen, Scorpions, M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce, Judas Priest, and others drew the largest audiences of the three-day event.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1983 and 1984, heavy metal went from an 8 percent to a 20 percent share of all recordings sold in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Several major professional magazines devoted to the genre were launched, including <i><a href="/wiki/Kerrang!" title="Kerrang!">Kerrang!</a></i> (in 1981) and <i><a href="/wiki/Metal_Hammer" title="Metal Hammer">Metal Hammer</a></i> (in 1984), as well as a host of fan journals. In 1985, <i>Billboard</i> declared, "Metal has broadened its audience base. Metal music is no longer the exclusive domain of male teenagers. The metal audience has become older (college-aged), younger (pre-teen), and more female".<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the mid-1980s, glam metal was a dominant presence on the U.S. charts, <a href="/wiki/Music_television" title="Music television">music television</a>, and the arena concert circuit. New bands such as L.A.\'s <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(American_band)" title="Warrant (American band)">Warrant</a> and acts from the East Coast like <a href="/wiki/Poison_(American_band)" title="Poison (American band)">Poison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(band)" title="Cinderella (band)">Cinderella</a> became major draws, while M\xc3\xb6tley Cr\xc3\xbce and Ratt remained very popular. Bridging the stylistic gap between hard rock and glam metal, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Bon_Jovi" title="Bon Jovi">Bon Jovi</a> became enormously successful with its third album, <i><a href="/wiki/Slippery_When_Wet" title="Slippery When Wet">Slippery When Wet</a></i> (1986). The similarly styled Swedish band <a href="/wiki/Europe_(band)" title="Europe (band)">Europe</a> became international stars with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Final_Countdown_(album)" title="The Final Countdown (album)">The Final Countdown</a></i> (1986). Its <a href="/wiki/The_Final_Countdown_(song)" title="The Final Countdown (song)">title track</a> hit number 1 in 25 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, MTV launched a show, <i><a href="/wiki/Headbanger%27s_Ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Headbanger&#39;s Ball">Headbanger\'s Ball</a></i>, devoted exclusively to heavy metal videos. However, the metal audience had begun to factionalize, with those in many underground metal scenes favoring more extreme sounds and disparaging the popular style as "light metal" or "hair metal".<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One band that reached diverse audiences was <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N&#39; Roses">Guns N\' Roses</a>. In contrast to their glam metal contemporaries in L.A., they were seen as much more raw and dangerous. With the release of their chart-topping <i><a href="/wiki/Appetite_for_Destruction" title="Appetite for Destruction">Appetite for Destruction</a></i> (1987), they "recharged and almost single-handedly sustained the Sunset Strip sleaze system for several years".<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The following year, <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane&#39;s Addiction">Jane\'s Addiction</a> emerged from the same L.A. hard-rock club scene with its major label debut, <i><a href="/wiki/Nothing%27s_Shocking" title="Nothing&#39;s Shocking">Nothing\'s Shocking</a></i>. Reviewing the album, <i>Rolling Stone</i> declared, "as much as any band in existence, Jane\'s Addiction is the true heir to Led Zeppelin".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The group was one of the first to be identified with the "<a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">alternative metal</a>" trend that would come to the fore in the next decade. Meanwhile, new bands such as New York\'s <a href="/wiki/Winger_(band)" title="Winger (band)">Winger</a> and New Jersey\'s <a href="/wiki/Skid_Row_(American_band)" title="Skid Row (American band)">Skid Row</a> sustained the popularity of the glam metal style.<sup id="cite_ref-Covach_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Covach-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Other_heavy_metal_genres:_1980s.2C_1990s.2C_and_2000s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_heavy_metal_genres:_1980s,_1990s,_and_2000s">Other heavy metal genres: 1980s, 1990s, and 2000s</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Suicidal_Tendencies_@_Capitol_(18_5_2011)_(5770925587).jpg" class="image"><img alt="The drummer from the band Suicidal Tendencies, Eric Moore, is shown behind his drumkit. One hand is raised with the index finger and pinky extended." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg/220px-Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg/330px-Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg/440px-Suicidal_Tendencies_%40_Capitol_%2818_5_2011%29_%285770925587%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="645" data-file-height="430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Suicidal_Tendencies_@_Capitol_(18_5_2011)_(5770925587).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drummer <a href="/wiki/List_of_Suicidal_Tendencies_band_members" title="List of Suicidal Tendencies band members">Eric Moore</a> from <a href="/wiki/Crossover_thrash" title="Crossover thrash">crossover thrash</a> band <a href="/wiki/Suicidal_Tendencies_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Suicidal Tendencies (band)">Suicidal Tendencies</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_heavy_metal_genres" class="mw-redirect" title="List of heavy metal genres">subgenres of heavy metal</a> developed outside of the commercial mainstream during the 1980s<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Crossover_thrash" title="Crossover thrash">crossover thrash</a>. Several attempts have been made to map the complex world of underground metal, most notably by the editors of <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">AllMusic</a>, as well as critic <a href="/wiki/Rockdetector" class="mw-redirect" title="Rockdetector">Garry Sharpe-Young</a>. Sharpe-Young\'s multivolume metal encyclopedia separates the underground into five major categories: <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">thrash metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">death metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">black metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_metal" title="Power metal">power metal</a>, and the related subgenres of <a href="/wiki/Doom_metal" title="Doom metal">doom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_metal" title="Gothic metal">gothic metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1990, a review in <i>Rolling Stone</i> suggested retiring the term "heavy metal" as the genre was "ridiculously vague".<sup id="cite_ref-rs-1990_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rs-1990-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The article stated that the term only fueled "misperceptions of rock &amp; roll bigots who still assume that five bands as different as <a href="/wiki/Ratt" title="Ratt">Ratt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extreme_(band)" title="Extreme (band)">Extreme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">Anthrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danzig_(band)" title="Danzig (band)">Danzig</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mother_Love_Bone" title="Mother Love Bone">Mother Love Bone</a>" sound the same.<sup id="cite_ref-rs-1990_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rs-1990-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thrash_metal">Thrash metal</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">thrash metal</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slayer,_The_Fields_of_Rock,_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="The band Slayer is shown at concert. From left to right are an electric guitarist, a bass player (also singing), an electric guitarists, and a drummer. The first guitarist and bassist have long hair. The right-most guitarist has a bald head. The drummer has two bass drums." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/240px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg" width="240" height="115" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/360px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/480px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slayer,_The_Fields_of_Rock,_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thrash metal band <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">Slayer</a> performing in 2007 in front of a wall of speaker stacks.</div></div></div>\n<p>Thrash metal emerged in the early 1980s under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">hardcore punk</a> and the new wave of British heavy metal,<sup id="cite_ref-GTM_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> particularly songs in the revved-up style known as <a href="/wiki/Speed_metal" title="Speed metal">speed metal</a>. The movement began in the United States, with <a href="/wiki/Bay_Area_thrash_metal" title="Bay Area thrash metal">Bay Area thrash metal</a> being the leading scene. The sound developed by thrash groups was faster and more aggressive than that of the original metal bands and their glam metal successors.<sup id="cite_ref-GTM_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GTM-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Low-register guitar riffs are typically overlaid with <a href="/wiki/Shred_guitar" title="Shred guitar">shredding</a> leads. Lyrics often express <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">nihilistic</a> views or deal with <a href="/wiki/Social_issues" class="mw-redirect" title="Social issues">social issues</a> using visceral, gory language. Thrash has been described as a form of "urban blight music" and "a palefaced cousin of rap".<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;float:right">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" title="File:Angel of Death clip.ogg">"Angel of Death"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_4" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="27.877346938776" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/30/Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (65 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="64727" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/3/30/Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg/Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="191792" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"><a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">Slayer</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/Angel_of_Death_(Slayer_song)" title="Angel of Death (Slayer song)">Angel of Death</a>", from <i><a href="/wiki/Reign_in_Blood" title="Reign in Blood">Reign in Blood</a></i> (1986), which features the fast, technically complex musicianship typical of thrash metal.</div></div></td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The subgenre was popularized by the "Big Four of Thrash": <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">Metallica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">Anthrax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">Megadeth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">Slayer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Three German bands, <a href="/wiki/Kreator" title="Kreator">Kreator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodom_(band)" title="Sodom (band)">Sodom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Destruction_(band)" title="Destruction (band)">Destruction</a>, played a central role in bringing the style to Europe. Others, including San Francisco Bay Area\'s <a href="/wiki/Testament_(band)" title="Testament (band)">Testament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exodus_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Exodus (band)">Exodus</a>, New Jersey\'s <a href="/wiki/Overkill_(band)" title="Overkill (band)">Overkill</a>, and Brazil\'s <a href="/wiki/Sepultura" title="Sepultura">Sepultura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarc%C3%B3fago" title="Sarc\xc3\xb3fago">Sarc\xc3\xb3fago</a>, also had a significant impact. Although thrash began as an underground movement, and remained largely that for almost a decade, the leading bands of the scene began to reach a wider audience. Metallica brought the sound into the top 40 of the <i>Billboard</i> album chart in 1986 with <i><a href="/wiki/Master_of_Puppets" title="Master of Puppets">Master of Puppets</a></i>, the genre\'s first platinum record.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Two years later, the band\'s <i><a href="/wiki/...And_Justice_for_All_(album)" title="...And Justice for All (album)">...And Justice for All</a></i> hit number 6, while Megadeth and Anthrax also had top 40 records on the American charts.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though less commercially successful than the rest of the Big Four, Slayer released one of the genre\'s definitive records: <i><a href="/wiki/Reign_in_Blood" title="Reign in Blood">Reign in Blood</a></i> (1986) was credited for incorporating heavier guitar <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbres</a>, and for including explicit depictions of death, suffering, violence and occult into thrash metal\'s lyricism.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Slayer attracted a following among <a href="/wiki/White_power_skinhead" title="White power skinhead">far-right skinheads</a>, and accusations of promoting violence and <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazi</a> themes have dogged the band.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Even though Slayer did not receive substantial media exposure, their music played a key role in the development of <a href="/wiki/Extreme_metal" title="Extreme metal">extreme metal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 1990s, thrash achieved breakout success, challenging and redefining the metal mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Metallica\'s <a href="/wiki/Metallica_(album)" title="Metallica (album)">self-titled 1991 album</a> topped the <i>Billboard</i> chart,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> as the band established international following.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Megadeth\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Countdown_to_Extinction" title="Countdown to Extinction">Countdown to Extinction</a></i> (1992) debuted at number two,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Anthrax and Slayer cracked the top 10,<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> and albums by regional bands such as Testament and Sepultura entered the top 100.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_metal">Death metal</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A man, Chuck Schuldiner, is shown on a dark shoreline. He has long hair, black pants and a black shirt, and a black leather jacket." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/150px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg" width="150" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/225px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/300px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="596" data-file-height="753" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">Death</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Schuldiner" title="Chuck Schuldiner">Chuck Schuldiner</a>, "widely recognized as the father of death metal".<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">death metal</a></div>\n<p>Thrash soon began to evolve and split into more extreme metal genres. "Slayer\'s music was directly responsible for the rise of death metal," according to MTV News.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The NWOBHM band Venom was also an important progenitor. The death metal movement in both North America and Europe adopted and emphasized the elements of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabolism" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabolism">diabolism</a> employed by such acts. Florida\'s <a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">Death</a>, San Francisco Bay Area\'s <a href="/wiki/Possessed_(band)" title="Possessed (band)">Possessed</a>, and Ohio\xe2\x80\x99s <a href="/wiki/Necrophagia" title="Necrophagia">Necrophagia</a><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> are recognized as seminal bands in the style. Both groups have been credited with inspiring the subgenre\'s name, the latter via its 1984 demo <i>Death Metal</i> and the song "Death Metal", from its 1985 debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Churches_(album)" title="Seven Churches (album)">Seven Churches</a></i> (1985). In the late 1980s and early 1990s, Swedish death metal became notable and melodic forms of death metal were created.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Death metal utilizes the speed and aggression of both thrash and hardcore, fused with lyrics preoccupied with <a href="/wiki/Z_movie" title="Z movie">Z-grade</a> <a href="/wiki/Slasher_film" title="Slasher film">slasher movie</a> violence and <a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">Satanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC27_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC27-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Death metal vocals are typically bleak, involving guttural "<a href="/wiki/Death_growl" title="Death growl">death growls</a>", high-pitched <a href="/wiki/Screaming_(music)" title="Screaming (music)">screaming</a>, the "death rasp",<sup id="cite_ref-VS_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VS-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> and other uncommon techniques.<sup id="cite_ref-Genre\xe2\x80\x94Death_Metal/Black_Metal_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genre\xe2\x80\x94Death_Metal/Black_Metal-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Complementing the deep, aggressive vocal style are downtuned, heavily <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Distortion (guitar)">distorted</a> guitars<sup id="cite_ref-LOC27_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC27-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VS_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VS-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> and extremely fast percussion, often with rapid <a href="/wiki/Bass_drum" title="Bass drum">double bass</a> drumming and "<a href="/wiki/Wall_of_sound" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall of sound">wall of sound</a>"\xe2\x80\x93style <a href="/wiki/Blast_beats" class="mw-redirect" title="Blast beats">blast beats</a>. Frequent tempo and <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature">time signature</a> changes and <a href="/wiki/Syncopation" title="Syncopation">syncopation</a> are also typical.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width:1px"></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" title="File:Obituary Suffocation.ogg">"Suffocation"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_5" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.969977324263" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/90/Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (99 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="98525" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/9/90/Obituary_Suffocation.ogg/Obituary_Suffocation.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="208344" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">"Suffocation" by <a href="/wiki/Obituary_(band)" title="Obituary (band)">Obituary</a> from the album <i><a href="/wiki/Slowly_We_Rot" title="Slowly We Rot">Slowly We Rot</a></i> (1989).</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Death metal, like thrash metal, generally rejects the theatrics of earlier metal styles, opting instead for an everyday look of ripped jeans and plain leather jackets.<sup id="cite_ref-MS28_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MS28-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> One major exception to this rule was <a href="/wiki/Deicide_(band)" title="Deicide (band)">Deicide</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Glen_Benton" title="Glen Benton">Glen Benton</a>, who branded an inverted cross on his forehead and wore armor on stage. <a href="/wiki/Morbid_Angel" title="Morbid Angel">Morbid Angel</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Neo-fascist" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-fascist">neo-fascist</a> imagery.<sup id="cite_ref-MS28_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MS28-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> These two bands, along with Death and <a href="/wiki/Obituary_(band)" title="Obituary (band)">Obituary</a>, were leaders of the major death metal scene that emerged in Florida in the mid-1980s. In the UK, the related style of <a href="/wiki/Grindcore" title="Grindcore">grindcore</a>, led by bands such as <a href="/wiki/Napalm_Death" title="Napalm Death">Napalm Death</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_Noise_Terror" title="Extreme Noise Terror">Extreme Noise Terror</a>, emerged from the <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-punk" title="Anarcho-punk">anarcho-punk</a> movement.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC27_222-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC27-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_metal">Black metal</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">black metal</a></div>\n<p>The first wave of black metal emerged in Europe in the early and mid-1980s, led by the United Kingdom\'s <a href="/wiki/Venom_(band)" title="Venom (band)">Venom</a>, Denmark\'s <a href="/wiki/Mercyful_Fate" title="Mercyful Fate">Mercyful Fate</a>, Switzerland\'s <a href="/wiki/Hellhammer" title="Hellhammer">Hellhammer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Frost" title="Celtic Frost">Celtic Frost</a>, and Sweden\'s <a href="/wiki/Bathory_(band)" title="Bathory (band)">Bathory</a>. By the late 1980s, Norwegian bands such as <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">Mayhem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burzum" title="Burzum">Burzum</a> were heading a second wave.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Black metal varies considerably in style and production quality, although most bands emphasize shrieked and growled vocals, highly distorted guitars frequently played with rapid <a href="/wiki/Tremolo_picking" class="mw-redirect" title="Tremolo picking">tremolo picking</a>, a dark atmosphere<sup id="cite_ref-Genre\xe2\x80\x94Death_Metal/Black_Metal_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genre\xe2\x80\x94Death_Metal/Black_Metal-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> and intentionally <a href="/wiki/Lo-fi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lo-fi">lo-fi</a> production, with ambient noise and background hiss.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Satanic themes are common in black metal, though many bands take inspiration from ancient <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a>, promoting a return to supposed pre-Christian values.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Numerous black metal bands also "experiment with sounds from all possible forms of metal, folk, classical music, electronica and avant-garde".<sup id="cite_ref-VS_223-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VS-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Darkthrone" title="Darkthrone">Darkthrone</a> drummer <a href="/wiki/Fenriz" title="Fenriz">Fenriz</a> explains, "It had something to do with production, lyrics, the way they dressed and a commitment to making ugly, raw, grim stuff. There wasn\'t a generic sound."<sup id="cite_ref-Campion_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campion-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although bands such as <a href="/wiki/Sarc%C3%B3fago" title="Sarc\xc3\xb3fago">Sarc\xc3\xb3fago</a> had been donning <a href="/wiki/Corpsepaint" class="mw-redirect" title="Corpsepaint">corpsepaint</a>, by 1990, Mayhem was regularly wearing <a href="/wiki/Corpsepaint" class="mw-redirect" title="Corpsepaint">corpsepaint</a>; many other black metal acts also adopted the look. Bathory inspired the <a href="/wiki/Viking_metal" title="Viking metal">Viking metal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">folk metal</a> movements and <a href="/wiki/Immortal_(band)" title="Immortal (band)">Immortal</a> brought blast beats to the fore. Some bands in the Scandinavian black metal scene became associated with considerable violence in the early 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> with Mayhem and Burzum linked to church burnings. Growing commercial hype around death metal generated a backlash; beginning in Norway, much of the Scandinavian metal underground shifted to support a black metal scene that resisted being co-opted by the commercial metal industry.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width:1px"></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg" title="File:Mayhem De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas.ogg">"De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas"</a></div>\n<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_6" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.921473922902" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/68/Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (130 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="129503" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/6/68/Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg/Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="204640" /></audio></div></div>\n<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">The title track of <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">Mayhem</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas" title="De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas">De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas</a></i> (1994).</div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>By 1992, black metal scenes had begun to emerge in areas outside Scandinavia, including Germany, France, and Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The 1993 murder of Mayhem\'s <a href="/wiki/Euronymous" title="Euronymous">Euronymous</a> by Burzum\'s <a href="/wiki/Varg_Vikernes" title="Varg Vikernes">Varg Vikernes</a> provoked intensive media coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-Campion_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campion-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Around 1996, when many in the scene felt the genre was stagnating,<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> several key bands, including Burzum and Finland\'s <a href="/wiki/Beherit_(band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Beherit (band)">Beherit</a>, moved toward an <a href="/wiki/Dark_ambient" title="Dark ambient">ambient</a> style, while <a href="/wiki/Symphonic_black_metal" title="Symphonic black metal">symphonic black metal</a> was explored by Sweden\'s <a href="/wiki/Tiamat_(band)" title="Tiamat (band)">Tiamat</a> and Switzerland\'s <a href="/wiki/Samael_(band)" title="Samael (band)">Samael</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a
